###begin article-title 0
###xml 75 83 75 83 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 131 138 131 138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
Eukaryotic Y-family polymerases bypass a 3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine analog in vitro and methyl methanesulfonate-induced DNA damage in vivo
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 0 16 0 16 <!--CREATIVE COMMONS-->
CREATIVE COMMONSThis is an Open Access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial License ( which permits unrestricted non-commercial use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 0 1 0 1 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 73 74 73 74 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 172 173 172 173 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 631 639 631 639 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 792 796 782 783 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#953;</bold>
###xml 819 843 802 826 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1002 1015 983 996 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1212 1216 1189 1190 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#953;</bold>
###xml 717 722 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 769 774 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 819 843 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1002 1015 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1189 1194 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
N3-methyl-adenine (3MeA) is the major cytotoxic lesion formed in DNA by SN2 methylating agents. The lesion presumably blocks progression of cellular replicases because the N3-methyl group hinders interactions between the polymerase and the minor groove of DNA. However, this hypothesis has yet to be rigorously proven, as 3MeA is intrinsically unstable and is converted to an abasic site, which itself is a blocking lesion. To circumvent these problems, we have chemically synthesized a 3-deaza analog of 3MeA (3dMeA) as a stable phosphoramidite and have incorporated the analog into synthetic oligonucleotides that have been used in vitro as templates for DNA replication. As expected, the 3dMeA lesion blocked both human DNA polymerases alpha and delta. In contrast, human polymerases eta, iota and kappa, as well as Saccharomyces cerevisiae poleta were able to bypass the lesion, albeit with varying efficiencies and accuracy. To confirm the physiological relevance of our findings, we show that in S. cerevisiae lacking Mag1-dependent 3MeA repair, poleta (Rad30) contributes to the survival of cells exposed to methyl methanesulfonate (MMS) and in the absence of Mag1, Rad30 and Rev3, human polymerases eta, iota and kappa are capable of restoring MMS-resistance to the normally MMS-sensitive strain.
###end p 2
###begin title 3
INTRODUCTION
###end title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 691 692 691 692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B1">1</xref>
DNA is subject to a variety of chemical modifications that alter its structure. Such alterations can block basic cellular functions such as transcription and/or replication and can lead to cell death, mutagenesis and cancer in higher eukaryotes. One such modification is DNA methylation, which can be caused by endogenous chemicals, products of metabolism, environmental exposure or treatment with several cancer chemotherapeutics. Not surprisingly, cells have developed several evolutionarily conserved mechanisms for repairing or tolerating this type of DNA damage, including base excision repair (BER), nucleotide excision repair (NER), recombination and translesion DNA synthesis (TLS) (1).
###end p 4
###begin p 5
###xml 131 132 131 132 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 138 139 138 139 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 210 211 210 211 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 252 253 252 253 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 278 279 278 279 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 299 300 299 300 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 490 491 478 479 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 513 514 501 502 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 741 742 729 730 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 748 749 736 737 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</sub>
###xml 771 772 759 760 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
Methylating agents primarily react with exocylic nitrogen or oxygen atoms on purines and pyrmidines, with the reaction mechanism (SN1 or SN2) determining the relative ratio of oxygen to nitrogen modifications (2). The major products in DNA exposed to SN2 methylating agents are N7-methylguanine and N3-methyladenine (3MeA), while there is very little methylation of oxygen atoms on the bases or the sugar phosphate backbone. 3MeA accounts for approximately20% of the base damage formed by SN2 methylating agents (2) and is considered to be the major cytotoxic lesion produced by such chemicals, based on the fact that bacterial and viral DNA polymerases are blocked before adenine residues but not guanine, on templates treated with either SN1 or SN2 methylating agents (3).
###end p 5
###begin p 6
###xml 134 137 134 137 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4 B5 B6 B7">4&#8211;7</xref>
###xml 361 362 361 362 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 376 377 376 377 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 388 394 388 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Aag&#8722;/&#8722;</italic>
###xml 580 587 580 587 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 703 711 703 711 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 752 753 752 753 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 860 867 860 867 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 918 924 918 924 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Aag&#8722;/&#8722;</italic>
###xml 1112 1113 1112 1113 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 140 145 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
3MeA is primarily removed by BER, although NER appears to provide an important back-up mechanism in the absence of BER in eukaryotes (4-7). Mouse embryonic fibroblasts (MEFs) lacking Aag, the DNA glycosylase that normally removes 3MeA from DNA, are sensitive to methyl methanesulfonate (MMS) and the compound methyl lexitropsin, which preferentially methylates N3 of adenine (8). Indeed, Aag-/- cells become arrested in S phase longer than their wild-type counterparts treated with either methylating agent, suggesting that the unrepaired 3MeA residues are a block to replication in vivo. However, it has been extremely difficult to prove that 3MeA blocks replication directly, as the half-life of 3MeA in vitro is estimated to be between 12 and 24 h (9), thereby precluding biochemical analysis. Furthermore, assuming 3MeA has a similar, or even faster decay in vivo, it seems likely that by the time the MMS-treated Aag-/- cells arrest in S phase, a significant portion of the 3MeA residues would be converted to replication-blocking abasic sites. The fact that the arrested cells eventually complete S phase (8) suggests that the replication-block is either removed by another repair mechanism, or that specialized DNA polymerases are able to bypass the damaged site.
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 160 162 157 159 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 163 165 160 162 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B11">11</xref>
###xml 247 249 235 237 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 456 457 444 445 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 505 507 493 495 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13">13</xref>
Several eukaryotic DNA polymerases are capable of performing TLS. Perhaps the best-characterized eukaryotic TLS polymerases are polzeta, a B-family polymerase (10,11), and poleta, poliota, polkappa and Rev1, all of which are Y-family polymerases (12). Based upon structural studies, the Y-family polymerases appear to be good candidates to facilitate TLS of 3MeA, since unlike high-fidelity replicative polymerases, they do not make the same contacts with N3 of adenine in the minor groove of duplex DNA (13).
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 66 74 66 74 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 450 474 442 466 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 539 547 531 539 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 571 579 563 571 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 732 762 715 733 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916; rev3&#916;</italic>
###xml 773 786 744 757 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 360 365 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 440 445 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 450 474 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 619 624 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 773 786 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
A major obstacle that has to date prevented the study of 3MeA TLS in vitro has been the inherent instability of the 3MeA lesion. To circumvent these problems, we have synthesized a stable 3-deaza analog of the nucleoside 3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine that can be incorporated into synthetic oligonucleotides as 3-deaza-3-methyladenine (3dMeA). Here, we show that human replicative polymerases polalpha and poldelta are blocked by 3dMeA, while human and Saccharomyces cerevisiae Y-family polymerases are capable of bypassing the modified base in vitro. In agreement with our in vitro observations, we also demonstrate that human DNA polymerases eta, iota and kappa have the ability to restore MMS-resistance to a normally MMS-sensitive mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strain of S. cerevisiae.
###end p 8
###begin title 9
MATERIALS AND METHODS
###end title 9
###begin title 10
Oligonucleotides
###end title 10
###begin p 11
###xml 116 124 116 124 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
Ethenoadenosine phosphoramidite was purchased from Glen Research (Sterling, VA, USA). All oligonucleotides used for in vitro replication and PCR assays, were synthesized by Lofstrand Labs Limited (Gaithersburg, MD, USA) and gel purified prior to use. Ethenoadenine and 3dMeA bases were incorporated into oligonucleotides using ultra-mild synthesis conditions.
###end p 11
###begin title 12
Enzymes
###end title 12
###begin p 13
###xml 16 18 12 14 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B14">14</xref>
###xml 34 36 27 29 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 51 53 42 44 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B16">16</xref>
###xml 59 72 50 63 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 98 100 86 88 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 221 234 201 214 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 59 72 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 141 146 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 205 210 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 221 234 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 309 314 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
Human poldelta (14), GST-poliota (15), His-poleta (16) and S. cerevisiae polzeta (GST-Rev3/Rev7) (17),were purified as previously described. Human polalpha was purchased from Chimerx (Milwaukee, WI, USA). Human polkappa, S. cerevisiae poleta and Rev1 protein were purchased from Enzymax (Lexington, KY, USA). Mouse Aag was purchased from Trevigen (Gaithersburg, MD, USA).
###end p 13
###begin title 14
Synthesis of the 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA-phosphoramidite
###end title 14
###begin p 15
A detailed protocol outlining the chemical synthesis of the 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA-phosphoramidite is available online as Supplementary Data.
###end p 15
###begin title 16
###xml 0 8 0 8 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vitro</italic>
In vitro Aag excision assay
###end title 16
###begin p 17
###xml 63 65 63 65 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 94 95 94 95 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</underline>
###xml 94 95 94 95 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><underline>A</underline></bold>
To measure DNA glycosylase activity on various substrates, 5'-[32P] 29mer, 5'-GCT CGT CAG ACG ATT TAG AGT CTG CAG TG-3' (with the adenine, ethenoadenine or 3dMeA underlined and in bold font), was annealed to its complementary strand. Double-stranded DNA of 0.4 pmol was treated with 3 U of mAag or mock treated for 1 h at 37degreesC. NaOH was added to a final concentration of 100 mM along with 10 mM Tris, 1 mM EDTA (final) and the samples were incubated at 37degreesC to cleave any resulting abasic sites. Samples were resolved on a 15% gel (8-M urea) and visualized with a Molecular Dynamics phosphorimager and ImageQuant software.
###end p 17
###begin title 18
Replication assays
###end title 18
###begin p 19
###xml 0 8 0 8 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vitro</italic>
###xml 94 95 94 95 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</underline>
###xml 94 95 94 95 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><underline>A</underline></bold>
###xml 290 292 290 292 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 412 419 412 419 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 426 428 426 428 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 591 593 591 593 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 739 740 738 739 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1033 1038 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In vitro replication assays were performed using the 29mer oligonucleotide 5'-GCT CGT CAG ACG ATT TAG AGT CTG CAG TG-3' as a template (with the location of the undamaged adenine, or 3dMeA underlined and in bold font). For most experiments described herein, this template was annealed to a [32P]-labeled 16mer primer with the following sequence; 5'-CAC TGC AGA CTC TAA A -3'. For the extension assays reported in Table 3, the [32P]-labeled primer was a 17mer with the sequence; 5'-CAC TGC AGA CTC TAA AX -3', where X is either A, or T. Primer-template DNAs were prepared by annealing the 5' [32P]-labeled primer to the unlabeled template DNA at a molar ratio of 1:1.5. Standard 10-mul reactions contained 40 mM Tris-HCl at pH 8.0, 5 mM MgCl2, 100 muM of each ultrapure dNTP (Amersham Pharmacia Biotech, NJ, USA), 10 mM DTT, 250 mug/ml BSA, 2.5% glycerol and 10 nM primer/template DNA. The concentration of polymerase added varied and is given in the legends to figures 3, 4, 5 and 7. After incubation at 37degreesC (or 30degreesC for yeast enzymes) for 5 min, reactions were terminated by the addition of 10 mul of 95% formamide/10 mM EDTA and the samples heated to 100degreesC for 5 min and briefly chilled on ice. Reaction mixtures (5 mul) were resolved on 15% polyacrylamide, 8M urea gels and analyzed with a Molecular Dynamics phosphorimager and ImageQuant software.
###end p 19
###begin title 20
Steady-state reaction conditions
###end title 20
###begin p 21
###xml 154 156 154 156 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
###xml 157 159 157 159 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 271 284 258 271 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1101 1108 1073 1080 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 202 207 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 271 284 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
For steady-state kinetic reactions, each polymerase was assayed to determine the amount of enzyme and nucleotide that would result in <20% incorporation (18,19): 0.4 U/reaction for polalpha, 1.2 nM for human poleta, 1.8 nM for poliota, 1.5 nM for polkappa and 1.4 nM for S. cerevisiae poleta. All reactions were performed in 10 mul in the standard reaction buffer described earlier, except those involving poliota, where the concentration of magnesium chloride was reduced from 5 to 0.25 mM. Reactions were initiated by the addition of the dNTP and lasted for 1.5-5 min for the correct nucleotides and 5-10 min for incorrect nucleotides, depending on the polymerase. On unmodified templates, dNTP concentrations ranged from 0.01 to 100 muM for the correct dTTP and from 1 to 500 muM for the incorrect dNTPs. For Y-family polymerases on the 3dMeA containing template, dTTP concentrations ranged from 0.1 muM to 1 mM while incorrect dNTPs ranged from 10 muM to 1 mM (except for poliota reactions where dATP ranged from 2 to 100 muM, while dGTP and dCTP ranged from 10 to 300 muM). For the data shown in Table 3, dCTP concentrations varied from 0.2 to 10 muM on the undamaged template and from 10 to 300 muM for 3dMeA-containing template. For polalpha with the 3dMeA-containing template, dATP and dTTP were varied from 0.1 to 1 mM. Replication products were separated on 15% polyacrylamide gels containing 8-M urea and visualized with a Molecular Dynamics phosphorimager and quantified with ImageQuant software.
###end p 21
###begin p 22
###xml 13 14 13 14 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">V</italic>
###xml 14 17 14 17 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">max</sub>
###xml 22 23 22 23 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>
###xml 23 24 23 24 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</sub>
###xml 156 158 156 158 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
###xml 242 243 242 243 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">V</italic>
###xml 243 246 243 246 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">max</sub>
###xml 247 248 247 248 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>
###xml 248 249 248 249 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</sub>
###xml 303 304 303 304 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">V</italic>
###xml 304 307 304 307 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">max</sub>
###xml 308 309 308 309 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>
###xml 309 310 309 310 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</sub>
###xml 311 320 311 320 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">incorrect</sub>
###xml 322 323 322 323 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">V</italic>
###xml 323 326 323 326 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">max</sub>
###xml 327 328 327 328 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>
###xml 328 329 328 329 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</sub>
###xml 330 337 330 337 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">correct</sub>
###xml 363 365 363 365 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
The apparent Vmax and Km values for each enzyme and nucleotide were determined from a Hanes-Woolf plot by linear least-squares fit as described previously (18). The catalytic efficiency of nucleotide insertion was calculated as the ration of Vmax/Km and the frequency of misinsertion was calculated as (Vmax/Km)incorrect/(Vmax/Km)correct as described previously (18) using SigmaPlot software (SPSS, Chicago, USA).
###end p 22
###begin title 23
###xml 14 19 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Generation of yeast strains and plasmids
###end title 23
###begin p 24
###xml 57 59 57 59 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 440 452 440 452 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD5 + mata)</italic>
###xml 454 456 454 456 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 527 536 527 532 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916;</italic>
###xml 597 617 593 605 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916;</italic>
###xml 664 669 652 653 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#916;</italic>
###xml 697 732 681 708 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916;::URA3 rad30&#916;:HIS3 mat</italic>
###xml 762 786 734 754 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rev3&#916;:HisG-URA3 mata</italic>
###xml 798 817 766 777 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rev3&#916;</italic>
###xml 834 839 794 795 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#916;</italic>
###xml 844 849 800 801 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#916;</italic>
###xml 883 913 835 853 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916; rev3&#916;</italic>
###xml 946 950 886 890 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAG1</italic>
###xml 955 959 895 899 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">REV3</italic>
###xml 996 1000 936 940 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 1124 1128 1064 1068 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 1218 1222 1158 1162 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAG1</italic>
###xml 1323 1327 1263 1267 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">REV3</italic>
###xml 4 9 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
All yeast strains were derived from the W303 background (20). MAG1 was disrupted by PCR amplification of the URA3 gene from pRS416 using primers with 40 nt of homology to upstream and downstream of MAG1 (MagUraF, 5'-ATG AAA CTA AAA AGG GAG TAT GAT GAG TTA ATA AAA GCA GCA GAG CAG ATT GTA CTG AGA GTG C-3' and MagUraR, 5'-TTA GGA TTT CAC GAA ATT TTC TTC TGC CTT CAT CAT GGC AGC GGT ATT TTC TCC TTA CGC-3') and transformed into C10-15a (W303 RAD5 + mata) (20). Positive disruptants were confirmed by PCR and MMS sensitivity. The mag1Delta haploid strain was mated to C10-10a, in order to obtain the mag1Delta rad30Delta double mutant (BPC1-4d) and a backcrossed mag1Delta (BPC1-2a) strain. BPC1-4d (mag1Delta::URA3 rad30Delta:HIS3 matalpha) was mated with C17-1A (rev3Delta:HisG-URA3 mata) to obtain mag1Delta rev3Delta (BPC2-8c), rad30Delta rev3Delta (BPC2-5a) double mutants and the mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta (BPC2-13c) triple mutant. Since MAG1 and REV3 disruptions were both marked by the URA3 gene, all strains genotypes were confirmed by PCR for these two genes by triplex PCR with the following reverse primer for URA3 (URA3_44R; 5'-ACT AGG ATG AGT AGC AGC ACG-3') and forward and reverse primers for either MAG1 (MAG1_95upF; 5'-TGG CCA CTG CCC TCT GAT ATG-3' and MAG1_298R; 5'-CTT GGC CAC TGA TCT GTT GAG-3') or REV3 (REV3_355upF; 5'-ACC ATT GTC CAA AGC TGT CGC-3' and REV3_223R; 5'-ACG TGG CAC AAT ACT TGA TGC C-3').
###end p 24
###begin p 25
###xml 30 43 30 43 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 129 133 129 133 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POLI</italic>
###xml 164 166 164 166 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 330 334 330 334 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POLH</italic>
###xml 387 389 387 389 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 453 457 453 457 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POLK</italic>
###xml 723 727 723 727 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POLK</italic>
###xml 787 817 787 817 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae RAD30</italic>
###xml 20 25 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 30 43 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 787 811 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
Plasmids expressing human and S. cerevisiae Y-family polymerases were constructed from pESC-LEU (Stratagene, La Jolla, CA, USA). POLI was cloned by digesting p6-1 (21) with NcoI, filling in the overhang with Klenow fragment, followed by digestion with AvaI and subsequent cloning into the SmaI site of pESC-LEU to generate pBP65. POLH was cloned as a NotI-BamHI fragment from pCDNA-XPV (22) into pESC-LEU digested with NotI and BglII to generate pPB66. POLK was cloned into pESC-LEU by first digesting pBP65 with NcoI, filling the ends with Klenow fragment to blunt end and subsequently digesting the vector with XmaI. An EcoRV-XmaI fragment from pHSE2 (a kind gift from Haruo Ohmori, University of Kyoto, Japan), encoding POLK was subsequently cloned into the vector to generate pBP98. Saccharomyces cerevisiae RAD30 was cloned as an NcoI-PstI fragment from pJM231 into the similarly digested plasmid, pBP65, to generate pBP82.
###end p 25
###begin title 26
Survival assays
###end title 26
###begin p 27
###xml 376 406 370 388 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916; rev3&#916;</italic>
###xml 545 558 518 531 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 665 666 636 637 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 983 984 954 955 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 67 72 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 446 451 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 479 484 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 511 516 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 545 558 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 580 585 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
MMS toxicity for each genotype was assessed on overnight cultures. Yeast were harvested and washed twice with PBS. MMS was diluted to 0.25% in PBS and aliquots of each strain were removed at selected time intervals, washed with PBS and diluted for plating on YPAD agar plates. Colonies were counted after 5 days at 30degreesC. For the complementation assays, strain BPC2-13A (mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta) was transformed with pBP65 (expresses human poliota), pBP66 (expresses human poleta), pBP98 (expresses human polkappa), pBP82 (expresses S. cerevisiae poleta) or pESC-LEU. Yeast strains were cultured overnight in complete synthetic raffinose medium lacking l-leucine. One hour prior to MMS treatment, the cultures were harvested by centrifugation and transferred to synthetic galactose medium to induce the expression of polymerases. Cells were harvested and treated as described above, except dilutions of each culture were plated on synthetic galactose agar plates lacking l-leucine.
###end p 27
###begin title 28
RESULTS
###end title 28
###begin title 29
###xml 28 29 28 29 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
3dMeA is a stable analog of N3-methyladenine
###end title 29
###begin p 30
###xml 31 39 31 39 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 85 87 85 87 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 392 400 392 400 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 457 458 457 458 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 595 603 595 603 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 688 697 688 697 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 1.</label>
###xml 760 761 760 761 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</bold>
###xml 872 873 872 873 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 957 958 957 958 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>
###xml 1198 1199 1198 1199 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 1200 1201 1200 1201 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 697 1302 697 1302 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="31">Synthesis of a synthetic 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite. (<bold>A</bold>) Chemical structures of 3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine and 3-deaza-3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine. Replacement of the <italic>N</italic>3 with carbon removed the positive charge and helps stabilize the glycosidic bond. (iii) Phosphitylation using <italic>N</italic>,<italic>N</italic>-diisopropylamino-(2-cyanoethyl)phosphoramidic chloride and diisopropylethylamine in dichloromethane.</p>
###xml 697 1302 697 1302 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="31">Synthesis of a synthetic 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite. (<bold>A</bold>) Chemical structures of 3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine and 3-deaza-3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine. Replacement of the <italic>N</italic>3 with carbon removed the positive charge and helps stabilize the glycosidic bond. (<bold>B</bold>) Schematic of the synthesis of the 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite (i) Di-benzoylation of 3-deaza-dA using benzoyl chloride in pyridine. (ii) Dimethoxytritylation using dimethoxytrityl chloride in pyridine. (iii) Phosphitylation using <italic>N</italic>,<italic>N</italic>-diisopropylamino-(2-cyanoethyl)phosphoramidic chloride and diisopropylethylamine in dichloromethane.</p></caption>
###xml 1302 1302 1302 1302 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn058f1"/>
###xml 688 1302 688 1302 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F1" position="float"><label>Figure 1.</label><caption><p textid="31">Synthesis of a synthetic 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite. (<bold>A</bold>) Chemical structures of 3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine and 3-deaza-3-methyl-2&#8242;-deoxyadenosine. Replacement of the <italic>N</italic>3 with carbon removed the positive charge and helps stabilize the glycosidic bond. (<bold>B</bold>) Schematic of the synthesis of the 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite (i) Di-benzoylation of 3-deaza-dA using benzoyl chloride in pyridine. (ii) Dimethoxytritylation using dimethoxytrityl chloride in pyridine. (iii) Phosphitylation using <italic>N</italic>,<italic>N</italic>-diisopropylamino-(2-cyanoethyl)phosphoramidic chloride and diisopropylethylamine in dichloromethane.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn058f1"/></fig>
3-Methyl adenosine is unstable in vitro with an estimated half-life of just 12-24 h (23). This short half-life has therefore limited biochemical or enzymatic studies on the lesion. To circumvent these problems, we have synthesized a 3-deaza-3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine analog of 3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine. The 3-deaza- analog has the same overall structure as the naturally occurring adduct (Figure 1A), but it lacks the positive charge associated with the N3 atom that normally destabilizes the glycosidic bond, and is therefore very stable. The analog can be synthesized as a phosphoramidite (Figure 1B) and can be incorporated into oligonucleotides by standard chemical DNA synthesis. Figure 1.Synthesis of a synthetic 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite. (A) Chemical structures of 3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine and 3-deaza-3-methyl-2'-deoxyadenosine. Replacement of the N3 with carbon removed the positive charge and helps stabilize the glycosidic bond. (B) Schematic of the synthesis of the 3-deaza-3-methyl-dA phosphoramidite (i) Di-benzoylation of 3-deaza-dA using benzoyl chloride in pyridine. (ii) Dimethoxytritylation using dimethoxytrityl chloride in pyridine. (iii) Phosphitylation using N,N-diisopropylamino-(2-cyanoethyl)phosphoramidic chloride and diisopropylethylamine in dichloromethane.
###end p 30
###begin p 31
###xml 63 64 63 64 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</bold>
###xml 175 176 175 176 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 260 261 260 261 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>
###xml 501 502 501 502 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 503 504 503 504 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>

###begin p 32
###xml 74 76 74 76 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B24">24</xref>
###xml 333 335 321 323 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25">25</xref>
###xml 353 361 341 349 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>
###xml 509 517 491 499 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>
###xml 804 813 780 789 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 2.</label>
###xml 1459 1461 1411 1413 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 813 1550 789 1502 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="33">3-deaza-3-methyl adenine is a stable analog of 3MeA. Mouse alkyladenine glycosylase (mAag) excises both 3-methyladenine (3MeA) and ethenodeoxyA (&#949;A). 0.4 pmol of undamaged, 3dMeA- or &#949;A-containing DNA was treated with 3 U of mAag, or mock treated for 1 h at 37&#176;C. To hydrolyze the resulting abasic sites, NaOH was added to a final concentration of 100 mM along with 10 mM Tris, 1 mM EDTA (final) and the samples were incubated at 37&#176;C. Samples were resolved on a 15% polyacrylamide gel containing 8-M urea. The nucleotide sequence of the 29mer duplex DNA is shown at the top of the panel and the position of the uncleaved <sup>32</sup>P-labeled 29mer oligonucleotide and 12mer product are shown on the right side of the gel.</p>
###xml 813 1550 789 1502 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="33">3-deaza-3-methyl adenine is a stable analog of 3MeA. ###xml 1550 1550 1502 1502 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn058f2"/>
###xml 804 1550 780 1502 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F2" position="float"><label>Figure 2.</label><caption><p textid="33">3-deaza-3-methyl adenine is a stable analog of 3MeA. Mouse alkyladenine glycosylase (mAag) excises both 3-methyladenine (3MeA) and ethenodeoxyA (&#949;A). 0.4 pmol of undamaged, 3dMeA- or &#949;A-containing DNA was treated with 3 U of mAag, or mock treated for 1 h at 37&#176;C. Samples were resolved on a 15% polyacrylamide gel containing 8-M urea. The nucleotide sequence of the 29mer duplex DNA is shown at the top of the panel and the position of the uncleaved <sup>32</sup>P-labeled 29mer oligonucleotide and 12mer product are shown on the right side of the gel.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn058f2"/></fig>
###xml 238 243 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 866 871 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
Since 3MeA is excised from DNA by the alkyladenine DNA glycosylase (Aag) (24), we determined if 3dMeA is also a substrate for Aag by treating either unmodified duplex DNA or DNA containing 3dMeA, or ethenoadenine (epsilonA) with purified mouse Aag followed by hydroxide treatment. epsilonA is a well-characterized substrate for Aag (25) and as noted in Figure 2, is completely excised from the substrate, as all of the epsilonA oligonucleotide is cleaved at the resulting abasic site, by hydroxide treatment (Figure 2). In contrast, the 3dMeA containing DNA shows relatively little cleaved substrate, and there is no detectable cleavage product in the unmodified control. This demonstrates that Aag can excise 3dMeA, but to a much lesser extent than epsilonA and presumably the naturally occurring 3MeA. Figure 2.3-deaza-3-methyl adenine is a stable analog of 3MeA. Mouse alkyladenine glycosylase (mAag) excises both 3-methyladenine (3MeA) and ethenodeoxyA (epsilonA). 0.4 pmol of undamaged, 3dMeA- or epsilonA-containing DNA was treated with 3 U of mAag, or mock treated for 1 h at 37degreesC. To hydrolyze the resulting abasic sites, NaOH was added to a final concentration of 100 mM along with 10 mM Tris, 1 mM EDTA (final) and the samples were incubated at 37degreesC. Samples were resolved on a 15% polyacrylamide gel containing 8-M urea. The nucleotide sequence of the 29mer duplex DNA is shown at the top of the panel and the position of the uncleaved 32P-labeled 29mer oligonucleotide and 12mer product are shown on the right side of the gel.
###end p 32
###begin p 33
###xml 646 648 622 624 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 53 58 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
3-deaza-3-methyl adenine is a stable analog of 3MeA. Mouse alkyladenine glycosylase (mAag) excises both 3-methyladenine (3MeA) and ethenodeoxyA (epsilonA). 0.4 pmol of undamaged, 3dMeA- or epsilonA-containing DNA was treated with 3 U of mAag, or mock treated for 1 h at 37degreesC. To hydrolyze the resulting abasic sites, NaOH was added to a final concentration of 100 mM along with 10 mM Tris, 1 mM EDTA (final) and the samples were incubated at 37degreesC. Samples were resolved on a 15% polyacrylamide gel containing 8-M urea. The nucleotide sequence of the 29mer duplex DNA is shown at the top of the panel and the position of the uncleaved 32P-labeled 29mer oligonucleotide and 12mer product are shown on the right side of the gel.
###end p 33
###begin p 34
###xml 547 549 547 549 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
Treatment with NaOH in the absence of Aag confirms that 3dMeA analog is indeed stable and that even boiling of the DNA to anneal the lesion containing strand to its complementary strand, did not result in abasic sites that could be subsequently hydrolyzed by treatment with NaOH. We suspect that the 3dMeA analog may not be removed as readily as naturally occurring 3MeA because of its stabilized glycosidic bond. Indeed, it has been proposed that the weakened glycosidic bond of several Aag substrates may facilitate excision by the glycosylase (26).
###end p 34
###begin title 35
3dMeA is a strong kinetic block to replicative polymerases, but is bypassed by Y-family polymerases
###end title 35
###begin p 36

###xml 112 117 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 772 777 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1288 1293 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1809 1814 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1871 1876 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 2328 2341 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
To date, there has been no direct evidence of 3MeA blocking a replicative DNA polymerase. We therefore compared human polalpha and poldelta to poleta, poliota, and polkappa in the presence of the four standard deoxynucleotides to determine which enzymes were capable of replicating a template containing 3dMeA. Under standard reaction conditions and with an undamaged template, each polymerases utilizes approximately10-20% of the primer (Figure 3A, left), however, virtually no extension of the primer annealed to the 3dMeA-containing template was observed in the presence of polalpha and poldelta, indicating that the lesion is a strong kinetic block to replicative polymerases. By comparison, both incorporation and bypass of the lesion was observed in the presence of human poleta, iota or kappa (Figure 3). Steady-state kinetic analyses revealed that incorporation of T opposite the 3dMeA lesion only occurred with an efficiency of 0.15-3% of that opposite an undamaged A (Table 1). However, when one compares the catalytic activity (Vmax/Km) of the Y-family enzymes ability to incorporate opposite the 3dMeA lesion, it is 125- to 1200-fold more efficient than the incorporation by polalpha (Table 1) (full kinetic parameters are supplied as Supplementary Data). Figure 3.Ability of human DNA polymerases to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in vitro. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 5 min at 37degreesC. Reactions contained 0.2 U polalpha, 5 nM poldelta, 3 nM poleta, 1.5 nM poliota and 3 nM polkappa. The nucleotide sequence of the template DNA is shown on the left-hand side of the gel. The 'A' in bold font is either undamaged (left-hand panel) or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine (3dMeA; right-hand panel). As clearly seen, the 3dMeA lesion is a strong block to replication by human DNA polymerases alpha and delta, but can be bypassed by human polymerases eta, iota and kappa.Table 1.Efficiency of insertion of T opposite undamaged A, or 3dMeA by various eukaryotic polymerasesPolymeraseaTemplateVmax/Km (muM-1 min-1)TemplateVmax/Km (muM-1 min-1)Efficiency of insertionbEfficiency of insertioncHs alphaA3.43dMeA0.00025.88 x 10-51Hs etaA3.063dMeA0.0732.30 x 10-2365Hs iotadA2103dMeA0.241.14 x 10-31200Hs kappaA13.733dMeA0.0251.82 x 10-3125Sc etaA0.963dMeA0.0253.02 x 10-2125aHs, Homo sapien; Sc, S. cerevisiae.bInsertion opposite 3dMeA relative to the insertion opposite an undamaged A.cVmax/Km opposite 3dMeA relative to the Vmax/Km opposite 3dMeA by Hs polalpha.dIn the presence of 0.25 mM MgCl2.
###end p 36
###begin p 37
###xml 68 76 68 76 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 11 16 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 532 537 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 594 599 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Ability of human DNA polymerases to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in vitro. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 5 min at 37degreesC. Reactions contained 0.2 U polalpha, 5 nM poldelta, 3 nM poleta, 1.5 nM poliota and 3 nM polkappa. The nucleotide sequence of the template DNA is shown on the left-hand side of the gel. The 'A' in bold font is either undamaged (left-hand panel) or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine (3dMeA; right-hand panel). As clearly seen, the 3dMeA lesion is a strong block to replication by human DNA polymerases alpha and delta, but can be bypassed by human polymerases eta, iota and kappa.
###end p 37

###begin p 39
###xml 0 1 0 1 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">a</sup>
###xml 1 3 1 3 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Hs</italic>
###xml 5 16 5 16 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Homo sapien</italic>
###xml 18 20 18 20 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Sc</italic>

###xml 68 76 65 73 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>

###xml 451 453 445 447 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 472 474 466 468 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 677 685 667 675 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 688 697 678 687 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 4.</label>
###xml 805 807 788 790 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 810 812 793 795 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 812 820 795 803 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1200 1201 1169 1170 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1221 1222 1190 1191 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1245 1246 1214 1215 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1468 1469 1430 1431 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1536 1537 1498 1499 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 697 1538 687 1500 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="44">Ability of human DNA polymerases &#948; and &#953; to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in the presence of low Mg<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup><italic>in vitro</italic>. Standard reactions contained 100 &#956;M all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 5 min at 37&#176;C. Reactions contained 5 nM pol&#948; and 4 nM pol&#953;. The nucleotide sequence of the template DNA is shown on the left-hand side of the gel. The &#8216;A&#8217; in bold font is either undamaged (left-hand panel) or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine (3dMeA; right-hand panel). Track 1, No dNTPs; Track 2, 0.25 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>; Track 3, 5 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>; Track 4, 0.25 mM MnCl<sub>2</sub>. As clearly seen, the 3dMeA lesion is a strong block to replication by human DNA polymerases &#948; even in the presence of Mn. In contrast, pol&#953;-dependent incorporation opposite the lesion is stimulated by 0.25 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub> and significant bypass is observed in the presence of 0.25 mM MnCl<sub>2</sub>.</p>
###xml 697 1538 687 1500 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="44">Ability of human DNA polymerases &#948; and &#953; to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in the presence of low Mg<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup><italic>in vitro</italic>. ###xml 1538 1538 1500 1500 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn058f4"/>
###xml 688 1538 678 1500 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F4" position="float"><label>Figure 4.</label><caption><p textid="44">Ability of human DNA polymerases &#948; and &#953; to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in the presence of low Mg<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup><italic>in vitro</italic>. In contrast, pol&#953;-dependent incorporation opposite the lesion is stimulated by 0.25 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub> and significant bypass is observed in the presence of 0.25 mM MnCl<sub>2</sub>.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn058f4"/></fig>
###xml 502 507 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 708 713 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1318 1323 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Very recently, we discovered that the catalytic activity of poliota in vitro is dramatically enhanced in the presence of low concentrations of Mg2+ or Mn2+ (27). Indeed, poliota-dependent incorporation opposite the 3dMeA lesion increased significantly when comparing primer extension in 0.25 mM versus 5 mM MgCl2 and lesion bypass was greatly stimulated in the presence of 0.25 mM MnCl2 (Figure 4). Similar to studies with other B-family polymerases (28), low levels of Mn2+ also appeared to stimulate human poldelta's activity in the primer extension assays with the undamaged template, as well as enable a small amount of incorporation and extension beyond the 3dMeA lesion (Figure 4). Figure 4.Ability of human DNA polymerases delta and iota to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in the presence of low Mg2+/Mn2+in vitro. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 5 min at 37degreesC. Reactions contained 5 nM poldelta and 4 nM poliota. The nucleotide sequence of the template DNA is shown on the left-hand side of the gel. The 'A' in bold font is either undamaged (left-hand panel) or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine (3dMeA; right-hand panel). Track 1, No dNTPs; Track 2, 0.25 mM MgCl2; Track 3, 5 mM MgCl2; Track 4, 0.25 mM MnCl2. As clearly seen, the 3dMeA lesion is a strong block to replication by human DNA polymerases delta even in the presence of Mn. In contrast, poliota-dependent incorporation opposite the lesion is stimulated by 0.25 mM MgCl2 and significant bypass is observed in the presence of 0.25 mM MnCl2.
###end p 43

Ability of human DNA polymerases delta and iota to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in the presence of low Mg2+/Mn2+in vitro. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 5 min at 37degreesC. Reactions contained 5 nM poldelta and 4 nM poliota. ###xml 414 418 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mice</span>
###xml 1378 1383 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 2263 2276 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 2906 2919 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Next, we examined the single nucleotide insertion profile opposite the 3dMeA lesion promoted by poleta, poliota and polkappa (Figure 5) and discovered that both poleta and poliota are error-prone, in that they readily misincorporate A opposite the 3dMeA lesion. Indeed, the ability of both polymerases to incorporate A opposite 3dMeA is consistent with the increase in A:T to T:A transversions observed in vivo in mice exposed to MMS (29). In contrast, polkappa appears to be fairly accurate, as it primarily inserts T opposite 3dMeA. Analysis of the steady-state kinetics for each enzyme (Table 2) reflects the results shown in Figure 5. Poliota and poleta misinsert A opposite 3dMeA with a frequency of 0.46 and 0.48 relative to incorporation of the correct base T, respectively. In contrast, polkappa is 10-fold more accurate than either poleta or poliota and misincorporates A opposite 3dMeA with a frequency of 0.04. Each polymerase appears to have higher than expected efficiency of inserting C, but this may simply occur as a consequence of the local sequence context, since the next 5' template base is a G. While at first glance all three of the Y-family polymerases appear to be error-prone, they are, in fact, more accurate than polalpha, which actually misincorporates A opposite 3dMeA 4-fold better than T, in the steady-state assays (Table 2). Figure 5.Ability of human DNA polymerases eta, iota and kappa to (mis)incorporate opposite 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs (4), or each nucleotide separately (G, A, T, C) and lasted for 5 min at 37degreesC. Reactions contained 4 nM poleta, 6 nM poliota and 4 nM polkappa. The nucleotide sequence of the template DNA is shown on the left-hand side of the gel. ###begin p 54
###xml 194 201 185 192 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 395 402 368 375 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 767 774 716 723 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 665 670 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Finally, we examined the ability of poleta, poliota and polkappa to extend from a base paired with 3dMeA. The primer terminus was either a 'correctly' paired T:3dMeA, or was an A:3dMeA mispair (Table 3). Both poleta and polkappa extended the correctly paired T:3dMeA primer terminus relatively well and did so with an efficiency of approximately8-10% of that compared to a normal T:A base-pair (Table 3). In contrast, poliota only extended the T:3dMeA primer with an efficiency of about 4% relative to an undamaged base-pair. Both poliota and polkappa extended the A:3dMeA mispair approximately3- to 4-fold less efficiently than the T:3dMeA base-pair. In contrast, human poleta actually extended the A:3dMeA mispair slightly better than the correctly paired T:3dMeA (Table 3).
###end p 54
###begin title 55
###xml 0 24 0 24 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 0 24 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
Saccharomyces cerevisiae poleta is important in tolerating MMS-induced damage in the absence of MAG1

###xml 40 45 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 175 188 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 284 308 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 571 584 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 623 628 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 707 720 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1716 1729 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1779 1792 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1935 1948 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 2077 2090 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 2208 2221 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Based on our in vitro findings with the human Y-family polymerases, we were eager to determine if Y-family polymerases play a role in tolerating 3MeA in vivo. We chose to use S. cerevisiae as a model because it has a limited number of DNA polymerases compared with higher eukaryotes. Saccharomyces cerevisiae poleta is encoded by the RAD30 gene, and it is reported that rad30Delta strains are somewhat sensitive to MMS (30,31). However, in the W303 background, a RAD30 disruption is not sensitive to MMS (Figure 6A). Mag1 is the only DNA glycosylase that repairs 3MeA in S. cerevisiae and disruption of the MAG1 gene makes yeast highly sensitive to methylating agents, such as MMS. In order to determine if S. cerevisiae poleta is involved in tolerating lesions normally repaired by Mag1 (i.e. 3MeA), we generated a rad30Delta mag1Delta strain. Interestingly, the double mutant is more sensitive to MMS than the mag1Delta strain, suggesting that poleta may help facilitate bypass of persisting 3MeA lesions in vivo (Figure 6A). Previous studies have shown that polzeta is responsible for most MMS-induced mutagenesis (32,33), and deletion of REV3 (encoding the catalytic subunit of polzeta) further sensitizes mag1Delta strains to MMS (Figure 6B). Therefore, it is possible that both poleta and polzeta are important for survival after treatment with MMS in a mag1Delta background (Figure 6B). Indeed, the triple mutant is significantly more sensitive than either double mutant (Figure 6B). This suggests that polzeta and poleta act in independent repair pathways to tolerate unrepaired base damage caused by MMS. Similar observations and conclusions were recently drawn by Johnson et al. (34). Figure 6.Survival of S. cerevisiae exposed to MMS. Exponentially growing strains of S. cerevisiae were exposed to 0.25% MMS for 10, 20 or 30 min, washed and subsequently plated on YPAD for 5 days at 30degreesC. (A) Disruption of REV3 makes S. cerevisiae mildly sensitive to MMS, but disruption of RAD30 has no observable effect on MMS sensitivity. (B) Disruption of MAG1 sensitizes S. cerevisiae to MMS, and disruption of RAD30 sensitizes the strain to MMS, indicating that the Rad30 encoded poleta helps protect S. cerevisiae from lesions that are normally repaired by Mag1. (C) Disruption of REV3 also sensitizes a mag1Delta strain to MMS, and the disruption of both RAD30 and REV3 synergistically enhances the lethality of MMS, indicating that poleta and polzeta may operate in separate pathways to repair lesions normally removed from the genome by Mag1. Three independent isolates were tested for each strain and standard deviations, which were all below 1 log of survival have been omitted for clarity.
###end p 56

###xml 670 674 658 662 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">REV3</italic>
###xml 12 25 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>

###begin p 58
###xml 38 51 38 51 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 120 133 120 133 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 209 217 201 209 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 219 227 211 219 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 255 268 245 258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 400 408 385 393 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 499 506 481 488 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 881 889 857 865 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 914 922 890 898 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 972 992 948 960 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916;</italic>
###xml 1001 1031 969 987 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mag1&#916; rad30&#916; rev3&#916;</italic>
###xml 1144 1153 1098 1107 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 7.</label>
###xml 1164 1177 1118 1131 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1229 1237 1183 1191 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1153 2014 1107 1942 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="59">Ability of <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic> DNA polymerases to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine <italic>in vitro</italic>. Standard reactions contained 100 &#956;M all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 20 min at 30&#176;C. Reactions contained 4 nM pol&#951;, 6 nM pol&#950; and 15 nM Rev1. The nucleotide sequence of the DNA templates is shown on the left-hand side of each gel. Left-hand panels: the &#8216;A&#8217; in bold font is either undamaged adenine or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine. As can be seen, pol&#951; bypasses the 3dMeA lesion much more efficiently than pol&#950;. Rev1 has negligible activity on either template, and did not appreciably stimulate the activity of pol&#950; on the 3dMeA template. Right-hand panels: both Rev1 and pol&#950; are catalytically active, as they are able to incorporate dCMP opposite undamaged G. Rev1 also stimulates pol&#950; in the presence of dCTP alone as well as in the presence of all 4 dNTPs.</p>

###xml 38 51 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 120 133 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 241 246 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 255 268 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1164 1177 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
To determine which TLS polymerases in S. cerevisiae are capable of bypassing unrepaired 3MeA, we assayed the ability of S. cerevisiae poleta, polzeta, Rev1 and polzeta in conjunction with Rev1 to bypass 3dMeA in vitro (Figure 7). Similar to human poleta, S. cerevisiae poleta bypasses the 3dMeA lesion reasonably efficiently. In contrast, polzeta exhibits a much weaker ability to bypass the lesion (Figure 7, left-hand panel). Rev1, a dCMP transferase that is necessary for the function of polzeta in vivo, also has minimal activity on either the undamaged A-template, or 3dMeA-containing template, as well as having little, to no stimulatory effect, on polzeta's ability to bypass the 3dMeA lesion. Rev1 is clearly catalytically active under our assay conditions, as the enzyme is able to insert C opposite an undamaged template G, as well as further stimulate polzeta activity (Figure 7, right-hand panel). Our in vitro data, combined with the MMS sensitivities of the mag1Delta rad30Delta and the mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strains therefore supports the idea that poleta replicates past unrepaired 3MeA lesions in the absence of Mag1. Figure 7.Ability of S. cerevisiae DNA polymerases to bypass 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine in vitro. Standard reactions contained 100 muM all 4 dNTPs and lasted for 20 min at 30degreesC. Reactions contained 4 nM poleta, 6 nM polzeta and 15 nM Rev1. The nucleotide sequence of the DNA templates is shown on the left-hand side of each gel. Left-hand panels: the 'A' in bold font is either undamaged adenine or 3-deaza-3-methyl adenine. As can be seen, poleta bypasses the 3dMeA lesion much more efficiently than polzeta. Rev1 has negligible activity on either template, and did not appreciably stimulate the activity of polzeta on the 3dMeA template. Right-hand panels: both Rev1 and polzeta are catalytically active, as they are able to incorporate dCMP opposite undamaged G. Rev1 also stimulates polzeta in the presence of dCTP alone as well as in the presence of all 4 dNTPs.
###end p 58

Human Y-family polymerases rescue the MMS sensitivity of mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strains of S. cerevisiae

###xml 807 1799 743 1695 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="62">Human Y-family polymerases can restore MMS-resistance to a normally MMS-sensitive <italic>rad30&#916; rev3&#916; mag1&#916;</italic> strain of <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic>. Exponentially growing strains of <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic> harboring plasmids expressing human polymerases &#951;, &#953; and &#954; or <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic> pol&#951; under the control of a galactose inducible promoter were induced in complete synthetic galactose media without leucine. Three independent isolates were tested for each strain and standard deviations, which were all below 1 log of survival, have been omitted for clarity. As clearly seen, human pol&#951; and &#953; can restore MMS-resistance to the normally MMS-sensitive strain, but the greatest effect was observed with human pol&#954;, which was as efficient as native <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic> pol&#951; in restoring MMS-resistance.</p>

###xml 120 125 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 202 207 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 231 243 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S.cerevisiae</span>
###xml 369 374 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 531 536 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 650 663 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 677 682 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 758 763 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 776 789 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 807 812 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 930 943 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 978 991 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1022 1027 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1063 1076 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1572 1577 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1701 1706 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1750 1763 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
The enhanced MMS sensitivity of the mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strain gave us an opportunity to test the ability of human poleta, poliota and polkappa to bypass alkylation damage in vivo. When each human polymerase (as well as S.cerevisiae poleta, as a control), was expressed from a galactose inducible promoter in the triple mutant, we discovered that all of the human polymerases rescued the MMS-sensitivity of the mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strain, albeit to varying degrees (Figure 8). Quite remarkably, expression of human polkappa confers MMS resistance on the mag1Delta rad30Delta rev3Delta strain to the same extent as overproducing S. cerevisiae poleta. Both human poleta and poliota also confer MMS-resistance, but to a lesser degree than human polkappa or S. cerevisiae poleta. Figure 8.Human Y-family polymerases can restore MMS-resistance to a normally MMS-sensitive rad30Delta rev3Delta mag1Delta strain of S. cerevisiae. Exponentially growing strains of S. cerevisiae harboring plasmids expressing human polymerases eta, iota and kappa or S. cerevisiae poleta under the control of a galactose inducible promoter were induced in complete synthetic galactose media without leucine. Media was removed, and cells were washed and exposed to 0.25% MMS for 10, 20 or 30 min, washed and subsequently plated on complete synthetic galactose plates lacking leucine for 5 days at 30degreesC. Three independent isolates were tested for each strain and standard deviations, which were all below 1 log of survival, have been omitted for clarity. As clearly seen, human poleta and iota can restore MMS-resistance to the normally MMS-sensitive strain, but the greatest effect was observed with human polkappa, which was as efficient as native S. cerevisiae poleta in restoring MMS-resistance.
###end p 61
###begin p 62
###xml 82 112 82 100 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad30&#916; rev3&#916; mag1&#916;</italic>
###xml 123 136 111 124 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
